pgjr10{_}1150{_}All that is meant by affectation and posing was a mere surface trick.
pgjr10{_}1151{_}The real man, Byron, and his poems are perfectly unconscious, as unconscious as the wind.
pgjr10{_}1152{_}The books which have lived and always will live have this unconsciousness in them, and what is manufactured, self-centred, and self-contemplative will perish.
pgjr10{_}1153{_}The world's literature is the work of men, who, to use Byron's own words -   "Strip off this fond and false identity;"   who are lost in their object, who write because they cannot help it, imperfectly or perfectly, as the case may be, and who do not sit down to fit in this thing and that thing from a commonplace book.
pgjr10{_}1154{_}Many novelists there are who know their art better than Charlotte Bronte, but she, like Byron--and there are more points of resemblance between them than might at first be supposed--is imperishable because she speaks under overwhelming pressure, self-annihilated, we may say, while the spirit breathes through her.
pgjr10{_}1155{_}The Byron "vogue" will never pass so long as men and women are men and women.
pgjr10{_}1156{_}Mr. Arnold and the critics may remind us of his imperfections of form, but Goethe is right after all, for not since Shakespeare have we had any one der ihm zu vergleichen ware.
pgjr10{_}1157{_}A SACRIFICE    A fatal plague devastated the city.
pgjr10{_}1158{_}The god had said that it would continue to rage until atonement for a crime had been offered by the sacrifice of a man.
pgjr10{_}1159{_}He was to be perfect in body; he must not desire to die because he no longer loved life, or because he wished for fame.
pgjr10{_}1160{_}A statue must not be erected to his memory; no poem must be composed for him; his name must not appear in the city's records.
pgjr10{_}1161{_}A few volunteers presented themselves, but none of them satisfied all the conditions.
pgjr10{_}1162{_}At last a young man came who had served as the model for the image of the god in his temple.
pgjr10{_}1163{_}There was no question, therefore, of soundness of limb, and when he underwent the form of examination no spot nor blemish was found on him.
pgjr10{_}1164{_}The priest asked him whether he was in trouble, and especially whether he was disappointed in love.
pgjr10{_}1165{_}He said he was in no trouble; that he was betrothed to a girl to whom he was devoted, and that they had intended to be married that month.
pgjr10{_}1166{_}"I am," he declared, "the happiest man in the city."
pgjr10{_}1167{_}The priest doubted and watched him that evening, but he saw him walking side by side with this girl, and the two were joyous as a youth and a maiden ought to be in the height of their passion.
pgjr10{_}1168{_}She sat down and sang to him he played to her, and they embraced one another tenderly at parting.
pgjr10{_}1169{_}The next morning was the day on which he was to be slain.
pgjr10{_}1170{_}There was an altar in front of the temple, and a great crowd assembled, ranked round the open space.
pgjr10{_}1171{_}At the appointed hour the priest appeared, and with him was the youth, holding his beloved by the hand, but she was blindfolded.
pgjr10{_}1172{_}He let go her hand, knelt down, and in a moment the sacrificial knife was drawn across his throat.
pgjr10{_}1173{_}His body was placed upon the wood, and the priest was about to kindle it when a flash from heaven struck it into a blaze with such heat that when the fire dropped no trace of the victim remained.
pgjr10{_}1174{_}The girl, too, had disappeared, and was never seen again.
pgjr10{_}1175{_}In accordance with the god's decree, no statue was erected, no poem was composed, and no entry was made in the city records.
pgjr10{_}1176{_}But tradition did not forget that the saviour of the city was he who survived in the great image on which the name of the god was inscribed.
pgjr10{_}1177{_}THE AGED TREE    An aged tree, whose companions had gone, having still a little sap in its bark and a few leaves which grew therefrom, prayed it might see yet another spring.
pgjr10{_}1178{_}Its prayer was granted:  and spring came, but the old tree had no leaves save one or two near the ground, and a great fungus fixed itself on its trunk.
pgjr10{_}1179{_}It had a dull life in its roots, but not enough to know that its moss and fungus were not foliage.
pgjr10{_}1180{_}It stood there, an unlovely mass of decay, when the young trees were all bursting.
pgjr10{_}1181{_}"That rotten thing," said the master, "ought to have been cut down long ago."
pgjr10{_}1182{_}CONSCIENCE    "Conscience," said I, "her conscience would have told her."
pgjr10{_}1183{_}"Yes," said my father.
pgjr10{_}1184{_}"The strongest amongst the many objections to the Roman Catholic doctrine of confession is that it weakens our dependence on the conscience.
pgjr10{_}1185{_}If we seek for an external command to do what ought to be done in obedience to that inward monitor, whose voice is always clear if we will but listen, its authority will gradually be lost, and in the end it will cease to speak."
pgjr10{_}1186{_}"Conscience," said my grandmother musingly (turning to my father).
pgjr10{_}1187{_}"You will remember Phyllis Eyre?
pgjr10{_}1188{_}She was one of my best friends, and it is now two years since she died, unmarried.
pgjr10{_}1189{_}She was once governess to the children of Sir Robert Walsh, but remained in the house as companion to Lady Walsh long after her pupils had grown up.
pgjr10{_}1190{_}She was, in fact, more than a companion, for Lady Walsh trusted her and loved her.
pgjr10{_}1191{_}She was by birth a lady; she had been well educated, and, like her mistress, she was devoutly and evangelically pious.
pgjr10{_}1192{_}She was also very handsome, and this you may well believe, for, as you know, she was handsome as an old woman, stately and erect, with beautiful, undimmed eyes.
pgjr10{_}1193{_}When Evelina Walsh, the eldest daughter, was about one and twenty, Charles Fysshe, the young heir to the Fysshe property, came to stay with her brother, and Phyllis soon discovered, or thought she discovered, that he was in love with Evelina.
pgjr10{_}1194{_}He seemed to court her society, and paid her attentions which could be explained on one hypothesis only.
pgjr10{_}1195{_}Phyllis was delighted, for the match in every way was most suitable, and must gladden the hearts of Evelina's parents.
pgjr10{_}1196{_}The young man would one day be the possessor of twenty thousand acres; he had already taken a position in the county, and his soul was believed to be touched with Divine grace.
pgjr10{_}1197{_}Evelina certainly was in love with him, and Phyllis was not backward in urging his claims.
pgjr10{_}1198{_}She congratulated herself, and with justice, that if the marriage should ever take place, it would be acknowledged that she had had a hand in it.
pgjr10{_}1199{_}It might even be doubted whether Evelina, without Phyllis's approval, would have permitted herself to indulge her passion, for she was by nature diffident, and so beset with reasons for and against when she had to make up her mind on any important matter, that a decision was always most difficult to her.
pgjr10{_}1200{_}"Charles stayed for about six weeks, and was then called home.
pgjr10{_}1201{_}He promised that he would pay another visit of a week in the autumn, when Sir Robert was to entertain the Lord Lieutenant and there were to be grand doings at the Hall.
pgjr10{_}1202{_}Conversation naturally turned upon him during his absence, and Phyllis, as usual, was warm in his praise.
pgjr10{_}1203{_}One evening, after she had reached her own room and had lain down to sleep, a strange apparition surprised her.
pgjr10{_}1204{_}It was something more than a suspicion that she herself loved Charles.
pgjr10{_}1205{_}She strove to rid herself of this intrusion:  she called to mind the difference in their rank; that she was five years his senior, and that if she yielded she would be guilty of treachery to Evelina.
pgjr10{_}1206{_}It was all in vain; the more she resisted the more vividly did his image present itself, and she was greatly distressed.
pgjr10{_}1207{_}What was the meaning of this outbreak of emotion, not altogether spiritual, of this loss of self-possession, such as she had never known before?
pgjr10{_}1208{_}Her usual remedies against evil thoughts failed her, and, worst of all, there was the constant suggestion that these particular thoughts were not evil.
pgjr10{_}1209{_}Hitherto, when temptation had attacked her, she was sure whence it came, but she was not sure now.
pgjr10{_}1210{_}It might be an interposition of Providence, but how would it appear to Evelina?
pgjr10{_}1211{_}I myself, my dears, have generally found that to resist the devil is not difficult if I am quite certain that the creature before me is the devil, but it does tax my wits sometimes to find out if he is really the enemy or not.
pgjr10{_}1212{_}When Apollyon met Christian he was not in doubt for an instant, for the monster was hideous to behold:  he had scales like a fish, wings like a dragon, feet like a bear, out of his belly came fire and smoke, and his mouth was as the mouth of a lion.
pgjr10{_}1213{_}After some parleying he cast his dreadful dart, but Christian, without more ado, put up his shield, drew his sword, and presently triumphed.
pgjr10{_}1214{_}If Satan had turned himself, from his head to his ankles, into a man, and had walked by Christian's side, and had talked with him, and had agreed with him in everything he had to say, the bear's claws might have peeped out, but Christian, instead of fighting, would have begun to argue with himself whether the evidence of the face or the foot was the stronger.
pgjr10{_}1215{_}He would have been just as likely to trust the face, and in a few moments he would have been snapped up and carried off to hell.
pgjr10{_}1216{_}To go on with my story:  the night wore on in sophistry and struggle, and no inner light dawned with the sun.
pgjr10{_}1217{_}Phyllis was much agitated, for in the afternoon Charles was to return, and although amidst the crowd of visitors she might be overlooked, she could not help seeing him.
pgjr10{_}1218{_}She did see him, but did not speak to him.
pgjr10{_}1219{_}He sat next to Evelina at dinner, who was happy and expectant.

pgjr10{_}1253{_}THE GOVERNESS'S STORY    In the year 1850 I was living as governess in the small watering-place S., on the south coast of England.
pgjr10{_}1254{_}Amongst my friends was a young doctor, B., who had recently come to the town.
pgjr10{_}1255{_}He had not bought a practice, but his family was known to one or two of the principal inhabitants, and he had begun to do well.
pgjr10{_}1256{_}He deserved his success, for he was skilful, frank, and gentle, and he did not affect that mystery which in his elder colleagues was already suspected to be nothing but ignorance.
pgjr10{_}1257{_}He was one of the early graduates of the University of London, and representative of the new school of medical science, relying not so much upon drugs as upon diet and regimen.
pgjr10{_}1258{_}I was one of his first patients.
pgjr10{_}1259{_}I had a severe illness lasting for nearly three months; he watched over me carefully and cured me.
pgjr10{_}1260{_}As I grew better he began to talk on other matters than my health when he visited me.
pgjr10{_}1261{_}We found that we were both interested in the same books:  he lent me his and I lent him mine.
pgjr10{_}1262{_}It is almost impossible, I should think, for a young man and a young woman to be friends and nothing more, and I confess that my sympathy with him in his admiration of the Elizabethan poets, and my gratitude to him for my recovery passed into affection.
pgjr10{_}1263{_}I am sure also that he felt affection for me.
pgjr10{_}1264{_}He became confidential, and told me all his history and troubles.
pgjr10{_}1265{_}There was one peculiarity in his conversation which was new to me:  he never talked down to me, and he was not afraid at times to discuss subjects that in the society to which I had been accustomed were prohibited.
pgjr10{_}1266{_}Not a word that was improper ever escaped his lips, but he treated me in a measure as if I were a man, and I was flattered that he should put me on a level with himself.
pgjr10{_}1267{_}It is true that sometimes I fancied he was so unreserved with me because he was sure he was quite safe, for I was poor.
pgjr10{_}1268{_}and although I was not ugly I was not handsome.
pgjr10{_}1269{_}However, on the whole, I was very happy in his society, and there was more than a chance that I should become his wife.
pgjr10{_}1270{_}After six months of our acquaintanceship had passed, M., an old schoolfellow of mine, took lodgings near me for the summer.
pgjr10{_}1271{_}She was a remarkable girl.
pgjr10{_}1272{_}If she was not beautiful, she was better-looking than I was, and she possessed a something, I know not what, more powerful than beauty to fascinate men.
pgjr10{_}1273{_}Perhaps it was her unconstrained naturalness.
pgjr10{_}1274{_}In walking, sitting, standing--whatever she did--her movements and attitudes were not impeded or unduly masked by artificial restrictions.
pgjr10{_}1275{_}I should not have called her profound, but what she said upon the commonest subjects was interesting, because it was so entirely her own.
pgjr10{_}1276{_}If she disliked a neighbour, she almost always disliked her for a reason which we saw, directly it was pointed out to us, to be just, but it was generally one which had not been given before.
pgjr10{_}1277{_}Her talk upon matters externally trivial was thus much more to me than many discourses upon the most important topics.
pgjr10{_}1278{_}On moral questions she expressed herself without any regard to prejudices.
pgjr10{_}1279{_}She did not controvert the authenticity of the ordinary standards, but nevertheless behaved as if she herself were her only law.
pgjr10{_}1280{_}The people in R., her little native borough, considered her to be dangerous, and I myself was once or twice weak enough to wonder that she held on a straight course with so little help from authority, forgetting that its support, in so far as it possesses any vital strength, is derived from the same internal source which supplied strength to her.
pgjr10{_}1281{_}When she came to S. she was unwell, and consulted my friend B.
pgjr10{_}1282{_}He did not at first quite like attending her, and she reported to me with great laughter how she had been told that he had made some inquiries about her from one of her neighbours at home with whom he happened to be acquainted, and how he had manoeuvred in his visits to get the servants or the landlady into the room.
pgjr10{_}1283{_}I met him soon afterwards, and he informed me that he had a new patient.
pgjr10{_}1284{_}When he heard that I knew her--I did not say how much I knew--he became inquisitive, and at last, after much beating about the bush, knitting his eyebrows and lowering his voice, he asked me whether I was aware that she was not quite--quite ABOVE SUSPICION!
pgjr10{_}1285{_}My goodness, how I flamed up!
pgjr10{_}1286{_}I defended her with vehemence:  I exaggerated her prudence and her modesty; I declared, what was the simple truth, that she was the last person in the world against whom such a scandalous insinuation should be directed, and that she was singularly inaccessible to vulgar temptation.
pgjr10{_}1287{_}I added that notwithstanding her seeming lawlessness she was not only remarkably sensitive to any accusation of bad manners, but that upon certain matters she could not endure even a joke.
pgjr10{_}1288{_}The only quarrel I remember to have had with her was when I lapsed into some commonplace jest about her intimacy with a music-master who gave her lessons.
pgjr10{_}1289{_}The way in which she took that jest I shall never forget.
pgjr10{_}1290{_}If I had made it to any other woman, I should have passed on, unconscious of anything inconsistent with myself, but she in an instant made me aware with hardly half a dozen words that I had disgraced myself.
pgjr10{_}1291{_}I was ashamed, not so much because I had done what was in the abstract wrong, but because it was something which was not in keeping with my real character.
pgjr10{_}1292{_}I hope it will not be thought that I am prosing if I take this opportunity of saying that the laws peculiar to each of us are those which we are at the least pains to discover and those which we are most prone to neglect.
pgjr10{_}1293{_}We think we have done our duty when we have kept the commandments common to all of us, but we may perhaps have disgracefully neglected it.
pgjr10{_}1294{_}Oh, how that afternoon with B. burnt itself into my memory for ever!

pgjr10{_}1296{_}He removed a few of the books, and I removed the others.

pgjr10{_}1314{_}I was condemned, I foresaw, henceforth to solitude, and that most terrible of all calamities, heart-starvation.

pgjr10{_}1322{_}We ought always, I suppose, to be glad when other people are happy, but God knows that sometimes it is very difficult to be so, and that their happiness is hard to bear.

pgjr10{_}1377{_}"You will go with me, then?"
pgjr10{_}1378{_}Again she kissed him.
pgjr10{_}1379{_}"It must not be."
pgjr10{_}1380{_}Before he could reply the train was stopping at the platform, and a gentleman with a lady appeared at the door.
pgjr10{_}1381{_}Miss Castleton stepped out and was at once driven away in a carriage with her companions.
pgjr10{_}1382{_}He lived three years and then died almost suddenly of the disease which he had foreseen would kill him.
pgjr10{_}1383{_}He had no children, but few relatives, and his attendant was a hospital nurse.
pgjr10{_}1384{_}But the day before his death a lady appeared who announced herself as a family friend, and the nurse was superseded.
pgjr10{_}1385{_}It was Elizabeth:  she came to his bedside, and he recognised her.
pgjr10{_}1386{_}"Not till this morning," she said, "did I hear you were ill."  "Happy," he cried, "though I die to-night."
pgjr10{_}1387{_}Soon afterwards--it was about sundown--he became unconscious; she sat there alone with him till the morning broke, and then he passed away, and she closed his eyes.
pgjr10{_}1388{_}ATONEMENT    "You ask me how I lost my foot?
pgjr10{_}1389{_}You I see that dog?"
pgjr10{_}1390{_}--an unattractive beast lying before the fire--"well, when I tell you how I came by him you will know how I lost it;" and he then related the following story:-  I was in Westmoreland with my wife and children for a holiday and we had brought our dog with us, for we knew he would be unhappy with the strangers to whom we had let our house.
pgjr10{_}1391{_}The weather was very wet and our lodgings were not comfortable; we were kept indoors for days together, and my temper, always irritable, became worse.
pgjr10{_}1392{_}My wife never resisted me when I was in these moods and the absence of opposition provoked me all the more.
pgjr10{_}1393{_}Had she stood up against me and told me I ought to be ashamed of myself it would have been better for me.
pgjr10{_}1394{_}One afternoon everything seemed to go wrong.
pgjr10{_}1395{_}A score of petty vexations, not one of which was of any moment, worked me up to desperation.
pgjr10{_}1396{_}I threw my book across the room, to the astonishment of my children, and determined to go out, although it was raining hard.
pgjr10{_}1397{_}My dog, a brown retriever, was lying on the mat just outside the door, and I nearly fell over him.
pgjr10{_}1398{_}"God damn you!"
pgjr10{_}1399{_}said I, and kicked him.
pgjr10{_}1400{_}He howled with pain, but, although he was the best of house-dogs and would have brought down any thief who came near him, he did not growl at me, and quietly followed me.
pgjr10{_}1401{_}I am not squeamish, but I was frightened directly the oath had escaped my lips.
pgjr10{_}1402{_}I felt as if I had created something horrible which I could not annihilate, and that it would wait for me and do me some mischief.
pgjr10{_}1403{_}The dog kept closely to my heels for about a mile and I could not make him go on in front.
pgjr10{_}1404{_}Usually the least word of encouragement or even the mere mention of his name would send him scampering with delight in advance.
pgjr10{_}1405{_}I began to think of something else, but in about a quarter of an hour I looked round and found he was not behind me.
pgjr10{_}1406{_}I whistled and called, but he did not come.
pgjr10{_}1407{_}In a renewed rage, which increased with every step I took, I turned back to seek him.
pgjr10{_}1408{_}Suddenly I came upon him lying dead by the roadside.
pgjr10{_}1409{_}Never shall I forget that shock--the reproach, the appeal of that poor lifeless animal!
pgjr10{_}1410{_}I stroked him, I kissed him, I whispered his name in his ear, but it was all in vain.
pgjr10{_}1411{_}I lifted up his beautiful broad paw which he was wont to lay on my knee, I held it between my hands, and when I let it go it fell heavily to the ground.
pgjr10{_}1412{_}I could not carry him home, and with bitter tears and a kind of dread I drew him aside a little way up the hill behind a rock.
pgjr10{_}1413{_}I went to my lodgings, returned towards dusk with a spade, dug his grave in a lonely spot near the bottom of a waterfall where he would never be disturbed, and there I buried him, reverently smoothing the turf over him.
pgjr10{_}1414{_}What a night that was for me!
pgjr10{_}1415{_}I was haunted incessantly by the vivid image of the dead body and by the terror which accompanies a great crime.
pgjr10{_}1416{_}I had repaid all his devotion with horrible cruelty.
pgjr10{_}1417{_}I had repented, but he would never know it.
pgjr10{_}1418{_}It was not the dog only which I had slain; I had slain Divine faithfulness and love.
pgjr10{_}1419{_}That GOD DAMN YOU sounded perpetually in my ears.
pgjr10{_}1420{_}The Almighty had registered and executed the curse, but it had fallen upon the murderer and not on the victim.
pgjr10{_}1421{_}When I rose in the morning I distinctly felt the blow of the kick in my foot, and the sensation lasted all day.
pgjr10{_}1422{_}For weeks I was in a miserable condition.
pgjr10{_}1423{_}A separate consciousness seemed to establish itself in this foot; there was nothing to be seen and no pain, but there was a dull sort of pressure of which I could not rid myself.
pgjr10{_}1424{_}If I slept I dreamed of the dog, and generally dreamed I was caressing him, waking up to the dreadful truth of the corpse on the path in the rain.
pgjr10{_}1425{_}I got it into my head--for I was half- crazy--that only by some expiation I should be restored to health and peace; but how to make any expiation I could not tell.
pgjr10{_}1426{_}Unhappy is the wretch who longs to atone for a sin and no atonement is prescribed to him!
pgjr10{_}1427{_}One night I was coming home late and heard the cry of "Fire!"
pgjr10{_}1428{_}I ran down the street and found a house in flames.
pgjr10{_}1429{_}The fire-escape was at the window, and had rescued a man, his wife and child.
pgjr10{_}1430{_}Every living creature was safe, I was told, save a dog in the front room on the ground-floor.
pgjr10{_}1431{_}I pushed the people aside, rushed in, half-blinded with smoke, and found him.
pgjr10{_}1432{_}I could not escape by the passage, and dropped out of the window into the area with him in my arms.
pgjr10{_}1433{_}I fell heavily on THAT foot, and when I was helped up the steps I could not put it to the ground.
pgjr10{_}1434{_}"You may have him for your pains," said his owner to me; "he is a useless cur.
pgjr10{_}1435{_}I wouldn't have ventured the singeing of a hair for him."
pgjr10{_}1436{_}"May I?"
pgjr10{_}1437{_}I replied, with an eagerness which must have seemed very strange.
pgjr10{_}1438{_}He was indeed not worth half a crown, but I drew him closely to me and took him into the cab.
pgjr10{_}1439{_}I was in great agony, and when the surgeon came it was discovered that my ankle was badly fractured.
pgjr10{_}1440{_}An attempt was made to set it, but in the end it was decided that the foot must be amputated.
pgjr10{_}1441{_}I rejoiced when I heard the news, and on the day on which the operation was performed I was calm and even cheerful.
pgjr10{_}1442{_}Our own doctor who came with the surgeon told him I had "a highly nervous temperament," and both of them were amazed at my fortitude.
pgjr10{_}1443{_}The dog is a mongrel, as you see, but he loves me, and if you were to offer me ten thousand golden guineas I would not part with him.
pgjr10{_}1444{_}LETTERS FROM MY AUNT ELEANOR {180} TO HER DAUGHTER SOPHIA, AND A FRAGMENT FROM MY AUNT'S DIARY.
pgjr10{_}1445{_}January 31, 1837.
pgjr10{_}1446{_}My Dearest Child,--It is now a month since your father died.
pgjr10{_}1447{_}It was a sore trial to me that you should have broken down, and that you could not be here when he was laid in his grave, but I would not for worlds have allowed you to make the journey.
pgjr10{_}1448{_}I am glad I forced you away.
pgjr10{_}1449{_}The doctor said he would not answer for the consequences unless you were removed.
pgjr10{_}1450{_}But I must not talk, not even to you.
pgjr10{_}1451{_}I will write again soon.
pgjr10{_}1452{_}Your most affectionate mother,  ELEANOR CHARTERIS.
pgjr10{_}1453{_}February 5, 1837.
pgjr10{_}1454{_}I have been alone in the library from morning to night every day.
pgjr10{_}1455{_}How foolish all the books look!
pgjr10{_}1456{_}There is nothing in them which can do me any good.
pgjr10{_}1457{_}He is NOT:  what is there which can alter that fact?
pgjr10{_}1458{_}Had he died later I could have borne it better.
pgjr10{_}1459{_}I am only fifty years old, and may have long to wait.
pgjr10{_}1460{_}I always knew I loved him devotedly; now I see how much I depended on him.
pgjr10{_}1461{_}I had become so knit up with him that I imagined his strength to be mine.
pgjr10{_}1462{_}His support was so continuous and so soft that I was unconscious of it.
pgjr10{_}1463{_}How clear-headed and resolute he was in difficulty and danger!
pgjr10{_}1464{_}You do not remember the great fire?
pgjr10{_}1465{_}We were waked up out of our sleep; the flames spread rapidly; a mob filled the street, shouting and breaking open doors.
pgjr10{_}1466{_}The man in charge of the engines lost his head, but your father was perfectly cool.
pgjr10{_}1467{_}He got on horseback, directed two or three friends to do the same; they galloped into the town and drove the crowd away.
pgjr10{_}1468{_}He controlled all the operations and saved many lives and many thousands of pounds.
pgjr10{_}1469{_}Is there any happiness in the world like that of the woman who hangs on such a husband?
pgjr10{_}1470{_}February 10, 1837.
pgjr10{_}1471{_}I feel as if my heart would break if I do not see you, but I cannot come to your Aunt's house just now.
pgjr10{_}1472{_}She is very kind, but she would be unbearable to me.
pgjr10{_}1473{_}Have patience:  the sea air is doing you good; you will soon be able to walk, and then you can return.
pgjr10{_}1474{_}O, to feel your head upon my neck!
pgjr10{_}1475{_}I have many friends, but I have always needed a human being to whom I was everything.
pgjr10{_}1476{_}To your father I believe I was everything, and that thought was perpetual heaven to me.
pgjr10{_}1477{_}My love for him did not make me neglect other people.
pgjr10{_}1478{_}On the contrary, it gave them their proper value.
pgjr10{_}1479{_}Without it I should have put them by.
pgjr10{_}1480{_}When a man is dying for want of water he cares for nothing around him.
pgjr10{_}1481{_}Satisfy his thirst, and he can then enjoy other pleasures.
pgjr10{_}1482{_}I was his first love, he was my first, and we were lovers to the end.
pgjr10{_}1483{_}I know the world would be dark to you also were I to leave it.
pgjr10{_}1484{_}Perhaps it is wicked of me to rejoice that you would suffer so keenly.
pgjr10{_}1485{_}I cannot tell how much of me is pure love and how much of me is selfishness.
pgjr10{_}1486{_}I remember my uncle's death.
pgjr10{_}1487{_}For ten days or so afterwards everybody in the house looked solemn, and occasionally there was a tear, but at the end of a fortnight there was smiling and at the end of a month there was laughter.
pgjr10{_}1488{_}I was but a child then, but I thought much about the ease and speed with which the gap left by death was closed.
pgjr10{_}1489{_}February 20, 1837.
pgjr10{_}1490{_}In a fortnight you will be here?
pgjr10{_}1491{_}The doctor really believes you will be able to travel?
pgjr10{_}1492{_}I am glad you can get out and taste the sea air.
pgjr10{_}1493{_}I count the hours which must pass till I see you.
pgjr10{_}1494{_}A short week, and then- -"the day after to-morrow, and the day after to-morrow of that day," and so I shall be able to reach forward to the Monday.
pgjr10{_}1495{_}It is strange that the nearer Monday comes the more impatient I am.
pgjr10{_}1496{_}March 3, 1837.
pgjr10{_}1497{_}With what sickening fear I opened your letter!
pgjr10{_}1498{_}I was sure it contained some dreadful news.
pgjr10{_}1499{_}You have decided not to come till Wednesday, because your cousin Tom can accompany you on that day.
pgjr10{_}1500{_}I KNOW you are quite right.
pgjr10{_}1501{_}It is so much better, as you are not strong, that Tom should look after you, and it would be absurd that you should make the journey two days before him.
pgjr10{_}1502{_}I should have reproved you seriously if you had done anything so foolish.

pgjr10{_}1505{_}Go straight to the library; I shall be there by myself.
pgjr10{_}1506{_}DIARY.
pgjr10{_}1507{_}January 1, 1838.--Three days ago she died.
pgjr10{_}1508{_}Henceforth there is no living creature to whom my existence is of any real importance.
pgjr10{_}1509{_}Crippled as she was, she could never have married.
pgjr10{_}1510{_}I might have held her as long as she lived.
pgjr10{_}1511{_}She could have expected no love but mine.
pgjr10{_}1512{_}God forgive me!
pgjr10{_}1513{_}Perhaps I did unconsciously rejoice in that disabled limb because it kept her closer to me.
pgjr10{_}1514{_}Now He has taken her from me.
pgjr10{_}1515{_}I may have been wicked, but has He no mercy?
pgjr10{_}1516{_}"I would speak to the Almighty, and I desire to reason with God."
pgjr10{_}1517{_}An answer in anger could better be borne than this impregnable silence.
pgjr10{_}1518{_}January 3rd.--A day of snow and bitter wind.
pgjr10{_}1519{_}There were very few at the grave, and I should have been better pleased if there had been none.
pgjr10{_}1520{_}What claim had they to be there?
pgjr10{_}1521{_}I have come home alone, and they no doubt are comforting themselves with the reflection that it is all over except the half-mourning.
pgjr10{_}1522{_}Her death makes me hate them.
pgjr10{_}1523{_}Mr. Maxwell, our rector, told me when my child was ill to remember that I had no right to her.
pgjr10{_}1524{_}"Right!"
pgjr10{_}1525{_}what did he mean by that stupid word?
pgjr10{_}1526{_}How trouble tries words!
pgjr10{_}1527{_}All I can say is that from her birth I had owned her, and that now, when I want her most, I am dispossessed.
pgjr10{_}1528{_}"Self, self"--I know the reply, but it is unjust, for I would have stood up cheerfully to be shot if I could have saved her pain.
pgjr10{_}1529{_}Doubly unjust, for my passion for her was a blessing to her as well as to me.
pgjr10{_}1530{_}January 6th.--Henceforth I suppose I shall have to play with people, to pretend to take an interest in their clothes and their parties, or, with the superior sort, to discuss politics or books.
pgjr10{_}1531{_}I care nothing for their rags or their gossip, for Lord Melbourne, Sir Robert Peel, or Mr. James Montgomery.
pgjr10{_}1532{_}I must learn how to take the tip of a finger instead of a hand, and to accept with gratitude comfits when I hunger for bread- -I, who have known--but I dare say nothing even to myself of my hours with him--I, who have heard Sophy cry out in the night for me; I, who have held her hand and have prayed by her bedside.
pgjr10{_}1533{_}January 10th.--I must be still.
pgjr10{_}1534{_}I have learned this lesson before--that speech even to myself does harm.
pgjr10{_}1535{_}If I admit no conversation nor debate with myself, I certainly will not admit the chatter of outsiders.
pgjr10{_}1536{_}Mr. Maxwell called again to-day.
pgjr10{_}1537{_}"Not a syllable on that subject," said I when he began in the usual strain.
pgjr10{_}1538{_}He then suggested that as this house was too large for me, and must have what he called "melancholy associations," I should move.
pgjr10{_}1539{_}He had suggested this before, when my husband died.
pgjr10{_}1540{_}How can I leave the home to which I was brought as a bride?
pgjr10{_}1541{_}how can I endure the thought that strangers are in our room, or in that other room where Sophy lay?
pgjr10{_}1542{_}Mr. Maxwell would think it sacrilege to turn his church into an inn, and it is a worse sacrilege to me to permit the profanation of the sanctuary which has been consecrated by Love and Death.
pgjr10{_}1543{_}I do not know what might happen to me if I were to leave.
pgjr10{_}1544{_}I have been what I am through shadowy nothings which other people despise.
pgjr10{_}1545{_}To me they are realities and a law.
pgjr10{_}1546{_}I shall stay where I am.
pgjr10{_}1547{_}"A villa," forsooth, on the outskirts of the town!
pgjr10{_}1548{_}My existence would be fractured:  it will at least preserve its continuity here.
pgjr10{_}1549{_}Across the square I can see the house in which I was born, and I can watch the shadow of the church in the afternoon slowly crossing the churchyard.
pgjr10{_}1550{_}The townsfolk stand in the street and go up and down it just as they did forty years ago--not the same persons, but in a sense the same people.
pgjr10{_}1551{_}My brother will call me extravagant if I remain here.
pgjr10{_}1552{_}He buys a horse and does not consider it extravagant, and my money is not wasted if I spend it in the only way in which it is of any value to me.
pgjr10{_}1553{_}January 12th.--I had thought I could be dumb, but I cannot.
pgjr10{_}1554{_}My sorrow comes in rushes.
pgjr10{_}1555{_}I lift up my head above the waves for an instant, and immediately I am overwhelmed--"all Thy waves and Thy billows have gone over me."
pgjr10{_}1556{_}My nights are a terror to me, and I fear for my reason.
pgjr10{_}1557{_}That last grip of Sophy's hand is distinctly on mine now, palpable as the pressure of a fleshly hand could be.
pgjr10{_}1558{_}It is strange that without any external circumstances to account for it, she and I often thought the same things at the same moment.
pgjr10{_}1559{_}She seemed to know instinctively what was passing in my mind, so that I was afraid to harbour any unworthy thought, feeling sure that she would detect it.
pgjr10{_}1560{_}Blood of my blood was she.
pgjr10{_}1561{_}She said "goodbye" to me with perfect clearness, and in a quarter of an hour she had gone.
pgjr10{_}1562{_}In that quarter of an hour there could not be the extinction of so much.
pgjr10{_}1563{_}Such a creature as Sophy could not instantaneously NOT BE.
pgjr10{_}1564{_}I cannot believe it, but still the volume of my life here is closed, the story is at an end; what remains will be nothing but a few notes on what has gone before.
pgjr10{_}1565{_}January 21st.--I went to church to-day for the first time since the funeral.
pgjr10{_}1566{_}Mr. Maxwell preached a dull, doctrinal sermon.
pgjr10{_}1567{_}Whilst my husband and Sophy lived, I was a regular attendant at church, and never thought of disputing anything I heard.
pgjr10{_}1568{_}It did not make much impression on me, but I accepted it, and if I had been asked whether I believed it, I should have said, "Certainly."
pgjr10{_}1569{_}But now a new standard of belief has been set up in me, and the word "belief" has a different meaning.
pgjr10{_}1570{_}February 3rd.--Whenever I saw anything beautiful I always asked Tom or Sophy to look.
pgjr10{_}1571{_}Now I ask nobody.
pgjr10{_}1572{_}Early this morning, after the storm in the night, the sky cleared, and I went out about dawn through the garden up to the top of the orchard and watched the disappearance of the night in the west.
pgjr10{_}1573{_}The loveliness of that silent conquest was unsurpassable.
pgjr10{_}1574{_}Eighteen months ago I should have run indoors and have dragged Tom and Sophy back with me.
pgjr10{_}1575{_}I saw it alone now, and although the promise in the slow transformation of darkness to azure moved me to tears, I felt it was no promise for me.
pgjr10{_}1576{_}March 1st.--Nothing that is PRESCRIBED does me any good.
pgjr10{_}1577{_}I cannot leave off going to church, but the support I want I must find out for myself.
pgjr10{_}1578{_}Perhaps if I had been born two hundred years ago, I might have been caught by some strong enthusiastic organisation and have been a private in a great army.
pgjr10{_}1579{_}A miserable time is this when each man has to grope his way unassisted, and all the incalculable toil of founders of churches goes for little or nothing.
pgjr10{_}1580{_}.
pgjr10{_}1581{_}.
pgjr10{_}1582{_}.
pgjr10{_}1583{_}I do not pray for any more pleasure:  I ask only for strength to endure, till I can lie down and rest.
pgjr10{_}1584{_}I have had more rapture in a day than my neighbours and relations have had in all their lives.
pgjr10{_}1585{_}Tom once said to me that he would sooner have had twenty-four hours with me as his wife than youth and manhood with any other woman he ever knew.
pgjr10{_}1586{_}He said that, not when we were first married, but a score of years afterwards.
pgjr10{_}1587{_}I remember the place and the hour.
pgjr10{_}1588{_}It was in the garden one morning in July, just before breakfast.
pgjr10{_}1589{_}It was a burning day, and massive white clouds were forming themselves on the horizon.
pgjr10{_}1590{_}The storm on that day was the heaviest I recollect, and the lightning struck one of our chimneys and dashed it through the roof.
pgjr10{_}1591{_}His passion was informed with intellect, and his intellect glowed with passion.
pgjr10{_}1592{_}There was nothing in him merely animal or merely rational.
pgjr10{_}1593{_}.
pgjr10{_}1594{_}.
pgjr10{_}1595{_}.
pgjr10{_}1596{_}To endure, to endure!
pgjr10{_}1597{_}Can there be any endurance without a motive?
pgjr10{_}1598{_}I have no motive.
pgjr10{_}1599{_}March 10th.--My sister and my brother-in-law came to-day and I wished them away.
pgjr10{_}1600{_}Now that my husband is dead I discover that the frequent visitors to our house came to see him and not me.
pgjr10{_}1601{_}There must be something in me which prevents people, especially women, from being really intimate with me.
pgjr10{_}1602{_}To be able to make friends is a talent which I do not possess, and if those who call on me are prompted by kindness only, I would rather be without them.
pgjr10{_}1603{_}The only attraction towards me which I value is that which is irresistible.
pgjr10{_}1604{_}Perhaps I am wrong, and ought to accept with thankfulness whatever is left to me if it has any savour of goodness in it.
pgjr10{_}1605{_}I have no right to compare and to reject.
pgjr10{_}1606{_}.
pgjr10{_}1607{_}.
pgjr10{_}1608{_}I provide myself with little maxims, and a breath comes and sweeps them away.
pgjr10{_}1609{_}What is permanent behind these little flickerings is black night: that is the real background of my life.
pgjr10{_}1610{_}April 24th.--I have been to London, and on Easter Sunday I went to High Mass at a Roman Catholic Church.
pgjr10{_}1611{_}I was obliged to leave, for I was overpowered and hysterical.
pgjr10{_}1612{_}Were I to go often my reason might be drowned, and I might become a devotee.
pgjr10{_}1613{_}And yet I do not think I should.
pgjr10{_}1614{_}If I could prostrate myself at a shrine I should want an answer.
pgjr10{_}1615{_}When I came out into the open air I saw again the PLAINNESS of the world:  the skies, the sea, the fields are not in accord with incense or gorgeous ceremonies.
pgjr10{_}1616{_}Incense and ceremonies are beyond the facts, and to the facts we must cleave, no matter how poor and thin they may be.
pgjr10{_}1617{_}May 5th.--If I am ill, I shall depend entirely on paid service.
pgjr10{_}1618{_}God grant I may die suddenly and not linger in imbecility.
pgjr10{_}1619{_}So much of me is dead that what is left is not worth preserving.
pgjr10{_}1620{_}Nearly everything I have done all my life has been done for love.
pgjr10{_}1621{_}I shall now have to act for duty's sake.
pgjr10{_}1622{_}It is an entire reconstruction of myself, the insertion of a new motive.
pgjr10{_}1623{_}I do not much believe in duty, nor, if I read my New Testament aright, did the Apostle Paul.
pgjr10{_}1624{_}For Jesus he would do anything.
pgjr10{_}1625{_}That sacred face would have drawn me whither the Law would never have driven me.
pgjr10{_}1626{_}May 7th.--It is painful to me to be so completely set aside.
pgjr10{_}1627{_}When Tom was alive I was in the midst of the current of affairs.
pgjr10{_}1628{_}Few men, except Maxwell, come to the house now.
pgjr10{_}1629{_}My property is in the hands of trustees.
pgjr10{_}1630{_}Tom continually consulted me in business matters.
pgjr10{_}1631{_}I have nothing to look after except my house, and I sit at my window and see the stream of life pass without touching me.
pgjr10{_}1632{_}I cannot take up work merely for the sake of taking it up.
pgjr10{_}1633{_}Nobody would value it, nor would it content me.
pgjr10{_}1634{_}How I used to pity my husband's uncle, Captain Charteris!
pgjr10{_}1635{_}He had been a sailor; he had fought the French; he had been in imminent danger of shipwreck, and from his youth upwards perpetual demands had been made upon his resources and courage.
pgjr10{_}1636{_}At fifty he retired, a strong, active man; and having a religious turn, he helped the curate with school-treats and visiting.
pgjr10{_}1637{_}He pined away and died in five years.
pgjr10{_}1638{_}The bank goes on.
pgjr10{_}1639{_}I have my dividends, but not a word reaches me about it.

pgjr10{_}1641{_}What a day this is!
pgjr10{_}1642{_}The turf is once more soft, the trees and hedges are washed, the leaves are turning yellow and are ready to fall.
pgjr10{_}1643{_}I have been sitting in the garden alone, reading the forty-ninth chapter of Genesis.
pgjr10{_}1644{_}I must copy the closing verses.
pgjr10{_}1645{_}It does me good to write them.
pgjr10{_}1646{_}"And Jacob charged them, and said unto them, I am to be gathered unto my people:  bury me with my fathers in the cave that is in the field of Ephron the Hittite, in the cave that is in the field of Machpelah, which is before Mamre, in the land of Canaan, which Abraham bought with the field of Ephron the Hittite for a possession of a burying-place.
pgjr10{_}1647{_}There they buried Abraham and Sarah his wife; there they buried Isaac and Rebekah his wife; and there I buried Leah.

pgjr10{_}1654{_}October 12th.--I do not wish to forget past joys, but I must simply remember them and not try to paint them.
pgjr10{_}1655{_}I must cut short any yearning for them.
pgjr10{_}1656{_}October 20th.--We do not say the same things to ourselves with sufficient frequency.
pgjr10{_}1657{_}In these days of book-reading fifty fine thoughts come into our heads in a day, and the next morning are forgotten.
pgjr10{_}1658{_}Not one of them becomes a religion.
pgjr10{_}1659{_}In the Bible how few the thoughts are, and how incessantly they are repeated!
pgjr10{_}1660{_}If my life could be controlled by two or three divine ideas, I would burn my library.
pgjr10{_}1661{_}I often feel that I would sooner be a Levitical priest, supposing I believed in my office, than be familiar with all these great men whose works are stacked around me.

pgjr10{_}1678{_}When she had been engaged to be married about a twelvemonth, she burned her face and the burn left a bad scar.
pgjr10{_}1679{_}Her lover found excuses for breaking off the engagement.

pgjr10{_}1701{_}CORRESPONDENCE BETWEEN GEORGE LUCY M.A., AND HIS GODCHILD, HERMIONE RUSSELL, B.A.
pgjr10{_}1702{_}My Dear Hermione,--I have sent you my little volume of verse translations into English, and you will find appended a few attempts at Latin and Greek renderings of favourite English poems.
pgjr10{_}1703{_}You must tell me what you think of them, and you must not spare a single blunder or inelegance.
pgjr10{_}1704{_}I do not expect any reviews, and if there should be none it will not matter, for I proposed to myself nothing more than my own amusement and that of my friends.
pgjr10{_}1705{_}I would rather have thoroughly good criticism from you than a notice, even if it were laudatory, from a magazine or a newspaper.
pgjr10{_}1706{_}You have worked hard at your Latin and Greek since we read Homer and Virgil, and you have had better instruction than I had at Winchester.
pgjr10{_}1707{_}These trifles were published about three months ago, but I purposely did not send you a copy then.
pgjr10{_}1708{_}You are enjoying your holiday deep in the country, and may be inclined to pardon that incurable old idler, your godfather and former tutor, for a waste of time which perhaps you would not forgive when you are teaching in London.
pgjr10{_}1709{_}Verse-making is out of fashion now.
pgjr10{_}1710{_}Goodbye.
pgjr10{_}1711{_}I should like to spend a week with you wandering through those Devonshire lanes if I could carry my two rooms with me and stick them in a field.
pgjr10{_}1712{_}Affectionately, G. L.   My Dear Godfather,--The little Musae came safely.
pgjr10{_}1713{_}My love to you for them, and for the pretty inscription.
pgjr10{_}1714{_}I positively refuse to say a single syllable on your scholarship.
pgjr10{_}1715{_}I have deserted my Latin and Greek, and they were never good enough to justify me in criticising yours.
pgjr10{_}1716{_}I have latterly turned my attention to Logic, History, and Moral Philosophy, and with the help of my degree I have obtained a situation as teacher of these sciences.
pgjr10{_}1717{_}I confess I do not regret the change.
pgjr10{_}1718{_}They are certainly of supreme importance.
pgjr10{_}1719{_}There is something to be learned about them from Latin and Greek authors, but this can be obtained more easily from modern writers or translations than by the laborious study of the originals.
pgjr10{_}1720{_}Do not suppose I am no longer sensible to the charm of classical art.
pgjr10{_}1721{_}It is wonderful, but I have come to the conclusion that the time spent on the classics, both here and in Germany, is mostly thrown away.
pgjr10{_}1722{_}Take even Homer.
pgjr10{_}1723{_}I admit the greatness of the Iliad and the Odyssey, but do tell me, my dear godfather, whether in this nineteenth century, when scores of urgent social problems are pressing for solution, our young people ought to give themselves up to a study of ancient legends?
pgjr10{_}1724{_}What, however, are Horace, Catullus, and Ovid compared with Homer?
pgjr10{_}1725{_}Much in them is pernicious, and there is hardly anything in them which helps us to live.
pgjr10{_}1726{_}Besides, we have surely enough in Chaucer, Spenser, Shakspeare, and Milton, to say nothing of the poets of this century, to satisfy the imagination of anybody.
pgjr10{_}1727{_}Boys spend years over the Metamorphoses or the story of the wars of AEneas, and enter life with no knowledge of the simplest facts of psychology.
pgjr10{_}1728{_}I look forward to a time not far distant, I hope, when our whole paedagogic system will be remodelled.
pgjr10{_}1729{_}Greek and Latin will then occupy the place which Assyrian or Egyptian hieroglyphic occupies now, and children will be directly prepared for the duties which await them.
pgjr10{_}1730{_}I have in preparation a book which I expect soon to publish, entitled Positive Education.
pgjr10{_}1731{_}It will appear anonymously, for society being constituted as it is, I am afraid that my name on the title-page would prevent me from finding employment.
pgjr10{_}1732{_}My object is to show how the moral fabric can be built up without the aid of theology or metaphysics.
pgjr10{_}1733{_}I profess no hostility to either, but as educational instruments I believe them to be useless.
pgjr10{_}1734{_}I begin with Logic as the foundation of all science, and then advance by easy steps (a) to the laws of external nature commencing with number, and (b) to the rules of conduct, reasons being given for them, with History and Biography as illustrations.
pgjr10{_}1735{_}One modern foreign language, to be learned as thoroughly as it is possible to learn it in this country, will be included.
pgjr10{_}1736{_}I desire to banish all magic in school training.
pgjr10{_}1737{_}Everything taught shall be understood.
pgjr10{_}1738{_}It is easier, and in some respects more advantageous, not to explain, but the mischief of habituating children to bow to the unmeaning is so great that I would face any inconvenience in order to get rid of it.
pgjr10{_}1739{_}All kinds of objections, some of them of great weight, may be urged against me, but the question is on which side do they preponderate?
pgjr10{_}1740{_}Is it no objection to our present system that the simple laws most necessary to society should be grounded on something which is unintelligible, that we should be brought up in ignorance of any valid obligation to obey moral precepts, that we should be unable to give any account of the commonest physical phenomena, that we should never even notice them, that we should be unaware, for example, of the nightly change in the position of planets and stars, and that we should nevertheless busy ourselves with niceties of expression in a dead tongue, and with tales about Jupiter and Juno?
pgjr10{_}1741{_}For what glorious results may we not look when children from their earliest years learn that which is essential, but which now, alas!
pgjr10{_}1742{_}is picked up unmethodically and by chance?
pgjr10{_}1743{_}I cannot help saying all this to you, for your Musae arrived just as my youngest brother came home from Winchester.
pgjr10{_}1744{_}He was delighted with it, for he is able to write very fair Latin and Greek.
pgjr10{_}1745{_}That boy is nearly eighteen.
pgjr10{_}1746{_}He does not know why the tides rise and fall, and has never heard that there has been any controversy as to the basis of ethics.
pgjr10{_}1747{_}Your affectionate godchild, HERMIONE.

pgjr10{_}1780{_}I should urge this on them perpetually, until at last, by association, lying would become impossible.

pgjr10{_}1903{_}Remember that the world is very big, and that there may be room in it for a few creatures like  Your affectionate godfather, G. L.    MRS. FAIRFAX    The town of Langborough in 1839 had not been much disturbed since the beginning of the preceding century.
pgjr10{_}1904{_}The new houses were nearly all of them built to replace others which had fallen into decay; there were no drains; the drinking-water came from pumps; the low fever killed thirty or forty people every autumn; the Moot Hall still stood in the middle of the High Street; the newspaper came but once a week; nobody read any books; and the Saturday market and the annual fair were the only events in public local history.
pgjr10{_}1905{_}Langborough, being seventy miles from London and eight from the main coach-road, had but little communication with the outside world.
pgjr10{_}1906{_}Its inhabitants intermarried without crossing from other stocks, and men determined their choice mainly by equality of fortune and rank.
pgjr10{_}1907{_}The shape of the nose and lips and colour of the eyes may have had some influence in masculine selection, but not much:  the doctor took the lawyer's daughter, the draper took the grocer's, and the carpenter took the blacksmith's.
pgjr10{_}1908{_}Husbands and wives, as a rule, lived comfortably with one another; there was no reason why they should quarrel.
pgjr10{_}1909{_}The air of the place was sleepy; the men attended to their business, and the women were entirely apart, minding their household affairs and taking tea with one another.
pgjr10{_}1910{_}In Langborough, dozing as it had dozed since the days of Queen Anne, it was almost impossible that any woman should differ so much from another that she could be the cause of passionate preference.
pgjr10{_}1911{_}One day in the spring of 1839 Langborough was stirred to its depths.
pgjr10{_}1912{_}No such excitement had been felt in the town since the run upon the bank in 1825, when one of the partners went up to London, brought down ten thousand pounds in gold with him by the mail, and was met at Thaxton cross-roads by a post-chaise, which was guarded into Langborough by three men with pistols.
pgjr10{_}1913{_}A circular printed in London was received on that spring day in 1839 by all the respectable ladies in the town stating that a Mrs. Fairfax was about to begin business in Ferry Street as a dressmaker.
pgjr10{_}1914{_}She had taken the only house to be let in Ferry Street.
pgjr10{_}1915{_}It was a cottage with a front and back sitting-room, and belonged to an old lady in Lincoln, who inherited it from her brother, who once lived in it but had been dead forty years.
pgjr10{_}1916{_}Before a week had gone by four-fifths of the population of Langborough had re-inspected it.
pgjr10{_}1917{_}The front room was the shop, and in the window was a lay-figure attired in an evening robe of rose-coloured silk, the like of which for style and fit no native lady had ever seen.
pgjr10{_}1918{_}Underneath it was a card-- "Mrs. Fairfax, Milliner and Dressmaker."
pgjr10{_}1919{_}The circular stated that Mrs. Fairfax could provide materials or would make up those brought to her by her customers.
pgjr10{_}1920{_}Great was the debate which followed this unexpected apparition.
pgjr10{_}1921{_}Who Mrs. Fairfax was could not be discovered.
pgjr10{_}1922{_}Her furniture and the lay- figure had come by the waggon, and the only information the driver could give was that he was directed at the "George and Blue Boar" in Holborn to fetch them from Great Ormond Street.
pgjr10{_}1923{_}After much discussion it was agreed that Mrs. Bingham, the wife of the wine merchant, should call on Mrs. Fairfax and inquire the price of a gown.
pgjr10{_}1924{_}Mrs. Bingham was at the head of society in Langborough, and had the reputation of being very clever.
pgjr10{_}1925{_}It was hoped, and indeed fully expected, that she would be able to penetrate the mystery.
pgjr10{_}1926{_}She went, opened the door, a little bell sounded, and Mrs. Fairfax presented herself.
pgjr10{_}1927{_}Mrs. Bingham's eyes fell at once upon Mrs. Fairfax's dress.
pgjr10{_}1928{_}It was black, with no ornament, and constructed with an accuracy and grace which proved at once to Mrs. Bingham that its maker was mistress of her art.
pgjr10{_}1929{_}Mrs. Bingham, although she could not entirely desert the linendraper's wife, whose husband was a good customer for brandy, had some of her clothes made in London when she stayed with her sister in town, and, to use her own phrase, "knew what was what."

pgjr10{_}2110{_}In speaking of the male creatures of the town we have left out Dr. Midleton.
pgjr10{_}2111{_}He was forty-eight years old, and had been rector twenty years.
pgjr10{_}2112{_}He had obtained high mathematical honours at Cambridge, and became a tutor in a grammar school, but was soon presented by his college with the living of Langborough.
pgjr10{_}2113{_}He was tall, spare, clean- shaven, grey-eyed, dark-haired, thin-faced, his lips were curved and compressed, and he stooped slightly.

pgjr10{_}2115{_}Tractarianism had not arisen in 1839, but he was High Church and an enemy to all kinds of fanaticism, apt to be satirical, even in his sermons, on the right of private judgment to interpret texts as it pleased in ignorance of Hebrew and Greek.

pgjr10{_}2121{_}The alms were allotted to serve political purposes, and the headmaster of the school enjoyed a salary of 800 pounds a year for teaching forty boys, of whom twenty were boarders.
pgjr10{_}2122{_}Mr. Midleton--he was Mr. Midleton then--very soon determined to alter this state of things.
pgjr10{_}2123{_}Jackson went about sneering at the newcomer who was going to turn the place upside down, and having been accustomed to interfere in the debates in the Board-room, interrupted the Rector at the third or fourth meeting.

pgjr10{_}2125{_}Chairman."

pgjr10{_}2130{_}The Rector was not popular with the middle class.

pgjr10{_}2578{_}You've--" but Mr. Bingham's strength again completely failed him, and he took a sudden turn--"You've taken a decided line lately at several of our meetings."
pgjr10{_}2579{_}The Doctor looked steadily at Mr. Bingham, who felt that every corner of his pitiful soul was visible.
pgjr10{_}2580{_}"The line I have taken you have generally supported.
pgjr10{_}2581{_}That is not what you mean.
pgjr10{_}2582{_}If I am defeated I shall be defeated by equivocating cowardice, and I shall consider myself honoured."
pgjr10{_}2583{_}The Doctor strode out of the room.
pgjr10{_}2584{_}He knew now that he was the common property of the town, and that every tongue was wagging about him and a woman, but he was defiant.
pgjr10{_}2585{_}The next morning he saw painted in white paint on his own wall -   "My dearly beloved, for all you're so bold, To-morrow you'll find you're left out in the cold; And, Doctor, the reason you need not to ax, It's because of a dressmaker--Mrs.
pgjr10{_}2586{_}F---fax."
pgjr10{_}2587{_}He was going out just as the gardener was about to obliterate the inscription.
pgjr10{_}2588{_}"Leave it, Robert, leave it; let the filthy scoundrels perpetuate their own disgrace."
pgjr10{_}2589{_}The result of the election was curious.
pgjr10{_}2590{_}Two of the Church candidates were returned at the top of the poll.
pgjr10{_}2591{_}Jem Casey came next.
pgjr10{_}2592{_}Dr. Midleton and the other two Radical and Dissenting candidates were defeated.
pgjr10{_}2593{_}There were between seventy and eighty plumpers for the two successful Churchmen, and about five-and-twenty split votes for them and Casey, who had distinguished himself by his coarse attacks on the Doctor.
pgjr10{_}2594{_}Mr. Bingham had a bad cold, and did not vote.
pgjr10{_}2595{_}On the following Sunday the church was fuller than usual.
pgjr10{_}2596{_}The Doctor preached on behalf of the Society for the Propagation of the Gospel.
pgjr10{_}2597{_}He did not allude directly to any of the events of the preceding week, but at the close of his sermon he said--"It has been frequently objected that we ought not to spend money on missions to the heathen abroad as there is such a field of labour at home.
pgjr10{_}2598{_}The answer to that objection is that there is more hope of the heathen than of many of our countrymen.
pgjr10{_}2599{_}This has been a nominally Christian land for centuries, but even now many deadly sins are not considered sinful, and it is an easier task to save the savage than to convince those, for example, whose tongue, to use the words of the apostle, is set on fire of hell, that they are in danger of damnation.
pgjr10{_}2600{_}I hope, therefore, my brethren, that you will give liberally."
pgjr10{_}2601{_}On Monday Langborough was amazed to find Mrs. Fairfax's shop closed.
pgjr10{_}2602{_}She had left the town.
pgjr10{_}2603{_}She had taken a post-chaise on Saturday and had met the up-mail at Thaxton cross-roads.
pgjr10{_}2604{_}Her scanty furniture had disappeared.
pgjr10{_}2605{_}The carrier could but inform Langborough that he had orders to deliver her goods at Great Ormond Street whence he brought them.
pgjr10{_}2606{_}Mrs. Bingham went to London shortly afterwards and called at Great Ormond Street to inquire for Mrs. Fairfax.
pgjr10{_}2607{_}Nobody of that name lived there, and the door was somewhat abruptly shut in her face.
pgjr10{_}2608{_}She came back convinced that Mrs. Fairfax was what Mrs. Cobb called "a bad lot."
pgjr10{_}2609{_}"Do you believe," said she, "that a woman who gives a false name can be respectable?
pgjr10{_}2610{_}We want no further proof."
pgjr10{_}2611{_}Nobody wanted further proof.
pgjr10{_}2612{_}No Langborough lady needed any proof if a reputation was to be blasted.
pgjr10{_}2613{_}"It's an alibi," said Mrs. Harrop.
pgjr10{_}2614{_}"That's what Tom Cranch the poacher did, and he was hung."
pgjr10{_}2615{_}"An alias, I believe, is the correct term," said Miss Tarrant.
pgjr10{_}2616{_}"It means the assumption of a name which is not your own, a most discreditable device, one to which actresses and women to whose occupation I can only allude, uniformly resort.
pgjr10{_}2617{_}How thankful we ought to be that our respected Rector's eyes must now be opened and that he has escaped the snare!
pgjr10{_}2618{_}It was impossible that he could be permanently attracted by vice and vulgarity.
pgjr10{_}2619{_}It is singular how much more acute a woman's perception often is than a man's.
pgjr10{_}2620{_}I saw through this creature at once."
pgjr10{_}2621{_}Eighteen months passed.
pgjr10{_}2622{_}The doctor one day was unpacking a book he had bought at Peterborough.
pgjr10{_}2623{_}Inside the brown paper was a copy of the Stamford Mercury, a journal which had a wide circulation in the Midlands.
pgjr10{_}2624{_}He generally read it, but he must have omitted to see this number.
pgjr10{_}2625{_}His eye fell on the following announcement--"On the 24th June last, Richard Leighton, aged 44 years."
pgjr10{_}2626{_}The notice was late, for the date of the paper was the 18th November.
pgjr10{_}2627{_}The next afternoon he was in London.
pgjr10{_}2628{_}He had been to Great Ormond Street before and had inquired for Mrs. Fairfax, but could find no trace of her.
pgjr10{_}2629{_}He now called again.
pgjr10{_}2630{_}"You will remember," he said, "my inquiry about Mrs. Fairfax:  can you tell me anything about Mrs.
pgjr10{_}2631{_}Leighton?"
pgjr10{_}2632{_}He put his hand in his pocket and pulled out five shillings.
pgjr10{_}2633{_}"She isn't here:  she went away when her husband died."
pgjr10{_}2634{_}"He died abroad?"
pgjr10{_}2635{_}"Yes."
pgjr10{_}2636{_}"Where has she gone?"
pgjr10{_}2637{_}"Don't know quite:  her friends wouldn't have anything to do with her.
pgjr10{_}2638{_}She said she was going to Plymouth.
pgjr10{_}2639{_}She had heard of something in the dressmaking line there."
pgjr10{_}2640{_}He handed over his five shillings, procured a substitute for next Sunday, and went to Plymouth.
pgjr10{_}2641{_}He wandered through the streets but could see no dressmaker's shop which looked as if it had recently changed hands.
pgjr10{_}2642{_}He walked backwards and forwards on the Hoe in the evening:  the Eddystone light glimmered far away on the horizon; and the dim hope arose in him that it might be a prophecy of success, but his hope was vain.
pgjr10{_}2643{_}It came into his mind that it was not likely that she would be there after dusk, and he remembered her preference for early exercise.
pgjr10{_}2644{_}The first morning was a failure, but on the second--it was sunny and warm--he saw her sitting on a bench facing the sea.
pgjr10{_}2645{_}He went up unobserved and sat down.
pgjr10{_}2646{_}She did not turn towards him till he said "Mrs.
pgjr10{_}2647{_}Leighton!"
pgjr10{_}2648{_}She started and recognised him.
pgjr10{_}2649{_}Little was spoken as they walked home to her lodgings, a small private house.
pgjr10{_}2650{_}On her way she called at a large shop where she was employed and obtained leave of absence until after dinner.
pgjr10{_}2651{_}"At last!"
pgjr10{_}2652{_}said the doctor when the door was shut.
pgjr10{_}2653{_}She stood gazing in silence at the dull red cinder of the dying fire.
pgjr10{_}2654{_}"You put the advertisement in the Stamford Mercury?"
pgjr10{_}2655{_}he said.
pgjr10{_}2656{_}"Yes."
pgjr10{_}2657{_}"I did not see it until a day or two ago."
pgjr10{_}2658{_}"I had better tell you at once.
pgjr10{_}2659{_}My husband, whom you knew, was convicted of forgery, and died at Botany Bay."
pgjr10{_}2660{_}Her eyes still watched the red cinders.
pgjr10{_}2661{_}The Doctor's countenance showed no surprise, for no news could have had any power over the emotion which mastered him.
pgjr10{_}2662{_}The long, slow years were fulfilled.
pgjr10{_}2663{_}Long and slow and the fulfilment late, but the joy it brought was the greater.
pgjr10{_}2664{_}Youthful passion is sweet, but it is not sweeter than the discovery when we begin to count the years which are left to us, and to fear there will be nothing in them better than in those which preceded them that for us also love is reserved.
pgjr10{_}2665{_}Mrs. Leighton was obliged to go back to her work in the afternoon, but she gave notice that night to leave in a week.
pgjr10{_}2666{_}In a couple of months Langborough was astounded at the news of the Rector's marriage with a Mrs. Leighton whom nobody in Langborough knew.
pgjr10{_}2667{_}The advertisement in the Stamford Mercury said that the lady was the widow of Richard Leighton, Esq., and eldest daughter of the late Marmaduke Sutton, Esq.
pgjr10{_}2668{_}Langborough spared no pains to discover who she was.
pgjr10{_}2669{_}Mrs. Bingham found out that the Suttons were a Devonshire family, and she ascertained from an Exeter friend that Mr. Marmaduke Sutton was the son of an Honourable, and that Mrs. Leighton was consequently a high-born lady.
pgjr10{_}2670{_}She had married as her first husband a man who had done well at Cambridge, but who took to gambling and drink, and treated her with such brutality that they separated.
pgjr10{_}2671{_}At last he forged a signature and was transported.
pgjr10{_}2672{_}What became of his wife afterwards was not known.
pgjr10{_}2673{_}Langborough was not only greatly moved by this intelligence, but was much perplexed.
pgjr10{_}2674{_}Miss Tarrant's estimate of the Doctor was once more reversed.
pgjr10{_}2675{_}She was decidedly of opinion that the marriage was a scandal.
pgjr10{_}2676{_}A woman who had consented to link herself with such a reprobate as the convict must have been from the beginning could not herself have possessed any reputation.
pgjr10{_}2677{_}Living apart, too, was next door to divorce, and who could associate with a creature who had been divorced?
pgjr10{_}2678{_}No doubt she was physically seductive, and the doctor had fallen a victim to her snares.
pgjr10{_}2679{_}Miss Tarrant, if she had not known so well what men are, would never have dreamed that Dr. Midleton, a scholar and a divine, could surrender to corporeal attractions.
pgjr10{_}2680{_}She declared that she could no longer expect any profit from his ministrations, and that she should leave the parish.
pgjr10{_}2681{_}Miss Tarrant's friends, however, did not go quite so far, and Mrs. Harrop confessed to Mrs. Cobb that "she for one wouldn't lay it down like Medes and Persians, that we should have nothing to do with a woman because her husband had made a fool of himself.
pgjr10{_}2682{_}I'm not a Mede nor a Persian, Mrs. Cobb.
pgjr10{_}2683{_}I say let us wait and see what she is like."
pgjr10{_}2684{_}Mrs. Bingham was of the same mind.
pgjr10{_}2685{_}She dwelt much to herself on the fact that Mrs. Midleton's great-grandfather must have been a lord.
pgjr10{_}2686{_}She secretly hoped that as a wine merchant's wife she might obtain admission into a "sphere," as she called it, from which the other ladies in the town might be excluded.
pgjr10{_}2687{_}Mrs. Bingham already foretasted the bliss of an invitation to the rectory to meet Lady Caroline from Thaxton Manor; she already foretasted the greater bliss of not meeting her intimate friends there, and that most exquisite conceivable bliss of telling them afterwards all about the party.
pgjr10{_}2688{_}Mrs. Midleton and her husband returned on a Saturday afternoon.
pgjr10{_}2689{_}The road from Thaxton cross-roads did not lie through the town:  the carriage was closed and nobody saw her.
pgjr10{_}2690{_}When they came to the rectory the Doctor pointed to the verse in white paint on the wall, "It shall be taken out," he said, "before to-morrow morning:  to-morrow is Sunday."
pgjr10{_}2691{_}He was expected to preach on that day and the church was crammed a quarter of an hour before the service began.
pgjr10{_}2692{_}At five minutes to eleven a lady and child entered and walked to the rector's pew.
pgjr10{_}2693{_}The congregation was stupefied with amazement.
pgjr10{_}2694{_}Mouths were agape, a hum of exclamations arose, and people on the further side of the church stood up.
pgjr10{_}2695{_}It was Mrs. Fairfax!
pgjr10{_}2696{_}Nobody had conjectured that she and Mrs. Leighton were the same person.
pgjr10{_}2697{_}It was unimaginable that a dressmaker should have had near ancestors in the peerage.
pgjr10{_}2698{_}It was more than a year and a half since she left the town.
pgjr10{_}2699{_}Mrs. Carter was able to say that not a single letter had been addressed to her, and she was almost forgotten.
pgjr10{_}2700{_}A few days afterwards Mrs. Sweeting had a little note requesting her to take tea with the Rector and his wife.
pgjr10{_}2701{_}Nobody was asked to meet her.
pgjr10{_}2702{_}Mrs. Bingham had called the day before, and had been extremely apologetic.
pgjr10{_}2703{_}"I am afraid, Mrs. Midleton, you must have thought me sometimes very rude to you."
pgjr10{_}2704{_}To which Mrs. Midleton replied graciously, "I am sure if you had been it would have been quite excusable."
pgjr10{_}2705{_}"Extremely kind of you to say so, Mrs.
pgjr10{_}2706{_}Midleton."
pgjr10{_}2707{_}Mrs. Cobb also called.
pgjr10{_}2708{_}"I'll just let her see," said Mrs. Cobb to herself; and she put on a gown which Mrs. Midleton as Mrs. Fairfax had made for her.
pgjr10{_}2709{_}"You'll remember this gown, Mrs.
pgjr10{_}2710{_}Midleton?"
pgjr10{_}2711{_}"Perfectly well.
pgjr10{_}2712{_}It is not quite a fit on the shoulders.
pgjr10{_}2713{_}If you will let me have it back again it will give me great pleasure to alter it for you."
pgjr10{_}2714{_}By degrees, however, Mrs. Midleton came to be loved by many people in Langborough.
pgjr10{_}2715{_}Mr. Sweeting not long afterwards died in debt, and Mrs. Sweeting, the old housekeeper being also dead, was taken into the rectory as her successor, and became Mrs. Midleton's trusted friend.
pgjr10{_}2716{_}Footnotes:  {10}  Since 1868 the Reminiscences and his Life have been published which put this estimate of him beyond all doubt.
